                return navigator.connection || navigator.mozConnection || navigator.webkitConnection || navigator.msConnection;

            function createLink(src) {
                var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");
                preloadLink.rel = "preload";
                preloadLink.href = src;
                preloadLink.as = "font";
                preloadLink.type = "font/woff2";
                preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
            }

            var connectionDetail = {
                saveDataEnabled: false,
                slowConnection: false
            };

            var connection = hasConnection();
            if (connection) {
                connectionDetail.saveDataEnabled = connection.saveData;
                if (/\slow-2g|2g/.test(connection.effectiveType)) {
                    connectionDetail.slowConnection = true;
                }
            }

            if (!(connectionDetail.saveDataEnabled || connectionDetail.slowConnection)) {
                createLink("/static/fonts/HardingText-Regular-Web-cecd90984f.woff2");

                document.documentElement.classList.add('save-data');

    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <script type="application/ld+json">{"mainEntity":{"headline":"Tuning local chemistry of P2 layered-oxide cathode for high energy and long cycles of sodium-ion battery","description":"Layered transition-metal oxides have attracted intensive interest for cathode materials of sodium-ion batteries. However, they are hindered by the limited capacity and inferior phase transition due to the gliding of transition-metal layers upon Na+ extraction and insertion in the cathode materials. Here, we report that the large-sized K+ is riveted in the prismatic Na+ sites of P2-Na0.612K0.056MnO2 to enable more thermodynamically favorable Na+ vacancies. The Mn-O bonds are reinforced to reduce phase transition during charge and discharge. 0.901 Na+ per formula are reversibly extracted and inserted, in which only the two-phase transition of P2 ↔ P’2 occurs at low voltages. It exhibits the highest specific capacity of 240.5 mAh g−1 and energy density of 654 Wh kg−1 based on the redox of Mn3+/Mn4+, and a capacity retention of 98.2% after 100 cycles. This investigation will shed lights on the tuneable chemical environments of transition-metal oxides for advanced cathode materials and promote the development of sodium-ion batteries. High-capacity and structural stable cathode materials are challenges for sodium-ion batteries. Here, the authors report a layered P2-Na0.612K0.056MnO2 with large-sized K+ riveted in the Na-layers to enable 0.9 Na+ (de)insertion with a reversible phase transition of     <meta name="journal_id" content="41467">
    <meta name="dc.title" content="Tuning local chemistry of P2 layered-oxide cathode for high energy and long cycles of sodium-ion battery">
    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2021 12:1">
    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html">
    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group">
    <meta name="dc.date" content="2021-04-15">
    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper">
    <meta name="dc.language" content="En">
    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2021 The Author(s)">
    <meta name="dc.rights" content="2021 The Author(s)">
    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com">
    <meta name="dc.description" content="Layered transition-metal oxides have attracted intensive interest for cathode materials of sodium-ion batteries. However, they are hindered by the limited capacity and inferior phase transition due to the gliding of transition-metal layers upon Na+ extraction and insertion in the cathode materials. Here, we report that the large-sized K+ is riveted in the prismatic Na+ sites of P2-Na0.612K0.056MnO2 to enable more thermodynamically favorable Na+ vacancies. The Mn-O bonds are reinforced to reduce phase transition during charge and discharge. 0.901 Na+ per formula are reversibly extracted and inserted, in which only the two-phase transition of P2 ↔ P’2 occurs at low voltages. It exhibits the highest specific capacity of 240.5 mAh g−1 and energy density of 654 Wh kg−1 based on the redox of Mn3+/Mn4+, and a capacity retention of 98.2% after 100 cycles. This investigation will shed lights on the tuneable chemical environments of transition-metal oxides for advanced cathode materials and promote the development of sodium-ion batteries. High-capacity and structural stable cathode materials are challenges for sodium-ion batteries. Here, the authors report a layered P2-Na0.612K0.056MnO2 with large-sized K+ riveted in the Na-layers to enable 0.9 Na+ (de)insertion with a reversible phase transition of P2-P’2.">

data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Fujun-Li-Aff1" data-author-popup="auth-Fujun-Li-Aff1" data-author-search="Li, Fujun" data-corresp-id="c1" data-track-context="researcher popup with no profile" data-track-index="11_12">Fujun Li<svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-mail-medium"></use></svg></a><span class="u-js-hide">&nbsp;

        <div class="c-article-body">
            <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Layered transition-metal oxides have attracted intensive interest for cathode materials of sodium-ion batteries. However, they are hindered by the limited capacity and inferior phase transition due to the gliding of transition-metal layers upon Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and insertion in the cathode materials. Here, we report that the large-sized K<sup>+</sup> is riveted in the prismatic Na<sup>+</sup> sites of P2-Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> to enable more thermodynamically favorable Na<sup>+</sup> vacancies. The Mn-O bonds are reinforced to reduce phase transition during charge and discharge. 0.901 Na<sup>+</sup> per formula are reversibly extracted and inserted, in which only the two-phase transition of P2 ↔ P’2 occurs at low voltages. It exhibits the highest specific capacity of 240.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and energy density of 654 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> based on the redox of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup>, and a capacity retention of 98.2% after 100 cycles. This investigation will shed lights on the tuneable chemical environments of transition-metal oxides for advanced cathode materials and promote the development of sodium-ion batteries.</p></div></div></section>

                <div class="main-content">
                    
                        <section data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as one promising next-generation battery system for large-scale energy storage and smart electric grid, due to the low cost and wide distribution of Na resources<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="House, R. A. et al. Superstructure control of first-cycle voltage hysteresis in oxygen-redox cathodes. Nature 577, 502–508 (2020)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e620">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, K. et al. Manganese based layered oxides with modulated electronic and thermodynamic properties for sodium ion batteries. Nat. Commun. 10, 5203 (2019)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e620_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Li, Q. et al. Both cationic and anionic co-(de)intercalation into a metal-oxide material. Joule 2, 1134–1145 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e623">3</a></sup>. The battery performance and cost are mainly governed by the applied cathode materials<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lee, G.-H. et al. Reversible anionic redox activities in conventional LiNi1/3Co1/3Mn1/3O2 Cathodes. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. 59, 8681–8688 (2020)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e627">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, Y. et al. Mitigation of Jahn-Teller distortion and Na+/vacancy ordering in distorted manganese oxide cathode material by Li substitution. Chem. Sci. 
                  https://doi.org/10.1039/D0SC05427E
                  
                 (2020)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e627_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ren, M., Fang, H., Wang, C., Li, H. &amp; Li, F. Advances on manganese-oxide-based cathodes for Na-ion batteries. Energy Fuels 34, 13412–13426 (2020)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e627_2">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Wang, C., Du, D., Song, M., Wang, Y. &amp; Li, F. A high-power Na3V2(PO4)3-Bi sodium-ion full battery in a wide temperature range. Adv. Energy Mater. 9, 1900022 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e630">7</a></sup>. A wide range of host materials, such as transition-metal oxides, phosphates, Prussian blue analogs, and so on, have been investigated for reversible (de)intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>. However, the intrinsic large ionic radius of 102 pm and molar weight of Na<sup>+</sup> over its Li<sup>+</sup> counterpart impose penalties on the specific capacity or the structure stability of cathode materials, which limit the deployment of SIBs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, P. F. et al. An abnormal 3.7 volt O3-type sodium-ion battery cathode. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. 57, 8178–8183 (2018)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e641">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhao, C. L. et al. Ti Substitution facilitating oxygen oxidation in Na2/3Mg1/3Ti1/6Mn1/2O2 cathode. Chem 5, 2913–2925 (2019)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e641_1">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="You, Y. et al. Insights into the improved high-voltage performance of Li-incorporated layered oxide cathodes for sodium-ion batteries. Chem 4, 2124–2139 (2018)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e641_2">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lei, K. et al. Dual interphase layers in situ formed on a manganese-based oxide cathode enable stable potassium storage. Chem 5, 3220–3231 (2019)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e641_3">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Li, X. et al. Direct visualization of the Jahn-Teller effect coupled to Na ordering in Na5/8MnO2. Nat. Mater. 13, 586–592 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e644">12</a></sup>. It is rational to design and synthesize cathode materials with more available sites for Na<sup>+</sup> storage and robust crystal structure for Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation, ensuring their large specific capacities and long cycle life.</p><p>Layered transition-metal oxides with earth-abundant elements have been actively studied as host materials for Na<sup>+</sup> storage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Vergnet, J., Saubanère, M., Doublet, M.-L. &amp; Tarascon, J.-M. The structural stability of P2-layered Na-based electrodes during anionic redox. Joule 4, 420–434 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e657">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kobayashi, W. et al. Thermal expansion in layered NaxMO2. Sci. Rep. 8, 3988 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e660">14</a></sup>. Sodium manganese oxide with a P2 phase (P2-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>) is of significant importance for SIBs, which is consisted of two MnO<sub>6</sub>-octahedra slabs and sandwiched Na layers in each unit cell with the ABBA oxygen packing frameworks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Zhao, C. L. et al. Revealing high Na-content P2-type layered oxide as advanced sodium-ion cathodes. J. Am. Chem. Soc. 142, 5742–5750 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e673">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Xiang, X., Zhang, K. &amp; Chen, J. Recent advances and prospects of cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries. Adv. Mater. 27, 5343–5364 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e676">16</a></sup>. It usually delivers a capacity of ~160 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to insertion of 0.60 Na<sup>+</sup> per formula for P2-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MnO<sub>2</sub><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Kumakura, S., Tahara, Y., Kubota, K., Chihara, K. &amp; Komaba, S. Sodium and manganese stoichiometry of P2-Type Na2/3MnO2. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. 55, 12760–12763 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e689">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Kulka, A. et al. Influence of Na/Mn arrangements and P2/P’2 phase ratio on the electrochemical performance of NaxMnO2 cathodes for sodium-ion batteries. J. Mater. Chem. A 8, 6022–6033 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e692">18</a></sup>. The limited movable Na<sup>+</sup> and phase transitions occurring upon its extraction and reinsertion are the major challenges. The Na<sup>+</sup> extraction to <i>x</i> &lt; 0.35 in P2-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> results in the irreversible P2 → OP4 phase transition, due to gliding of the MnO<sub>2</sub>-slabs, and consequently decaying capacities in cycles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Clément, R. J. et al. Structurally stable Mg-doped P2-Na2/3Mn1-yMgyO2 sodium-ion battery cathodes with high rate performance: insights from electrochemical, NMR and diffraction studies. Energy Environ. Sci. 9, 3240–3251 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e713">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Kumakura, S., Tahara, Y., Sato, S., Kubota, K. &amp; Komaba, S. P’2-Na2/3Mn0.9Me0.1O2 (Me = Mg, Ti, Co, Ni, Cu, and Zn): correlation between orthorhombic distortion and electrochemical property. Chem. Mater. 29, 8958–8962 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e716">20</a></sup>. Fe-doping in the MnO<sub>2</sub>-slabs of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is beneficial for a high capacity of 200 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> but with 35% capacity loss after 30 cycles, which is originated from the P2 → O2/OP4 phase transition<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Yabuuchi, N. et al. P2-type Nax[Fe1/2Mn1/2]O2 made from earth-abundant elements for rechargeable Na batteries. Nat. Mater. 11, 512–517 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e735">21</a></sup>. The capacity can also be promoted to 185 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> by co-doping Co and Ni for P2-Na<sub>0.7</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Ni<sub>0.3</sub>Co<sub>0.1</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (Yoshida et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Yoshida, J. et al. New P2-Na0.70Mn0.60Ni0.30Co0.10O2 layered oxide as electrode material for Na-ion batteries. J. Electrochem. Soc. 161, A1987–A1991 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e752">22</a></sup>). Partial Mg substitution for Mn in P2-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> delays the formation of OP4 phase, leading to smooth electrochemical profiles and extended cycling performance with a capacity of 106 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Clément et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Clément, R. J. et al. Structurally stable Mg-doped P2-Na2/3Mn1-yMgyO2 sodium-ion battery cathodes with high rate performance: insights from electrochemical, NMR and diffraction studies. Energy Environ. Sci. 9, 3240–3251 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e765">19</a></sup>). The substitution of Li or Al in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> can suppress the Jahn-Teller distortion and enhance its phase stability<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Paulsena, J. M. &amp; Dahn, J. R. Studies of the layered manganese bronzes, Na2/3[Mn1-xMx]O2 with M=Co, Ni, Li, and Li2/3[Mn1-xMx]O2 prepared by ion-exchange. Solid State Ion. 126, 3–24 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e775">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Pang, W. et al. P2-type Na2/3Mn1-xAlxO2 cathode material for sodium-ion batteries: Al-doped enhanced electrochemical properties and studies on the electrode kinetics. J. Power Sources 356, 80–88 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e778">24</a></sup>. It is clear that finely tuning the chemical environments of the MnO<sub>6</sub>-octahedra with suitable dopants in the place of Mn allows for either more accessible Na<sup>+</sup> sites for large capacities with limited cycles or minimized slab gliding for long cycles with moderate capacities. However, solutions to such paradox are challenging and highly desirable for the development of SIBs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Wang, P.-F., You, Y., Yin, Y.-X. &amp; Guo, Y.-G. Layered oxide cathodes for sodium-ion batteries: phase transition, air stability, and performance. Adv. Energy Mater. 8, 1701912 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e787">25</a></sup>.</p><p>Here, we show that the large-sized K<sup>+</sup> of 138 pm is riveted in the edge-sharing sites of Na<sup>+</sup> in P2-Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, which leads to the small interslab distance of 0.56 nm in Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and results in high diffusion barrier of K<sup>+</sup> and hence its immobility. The Na-O bond strength and Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy formation energy are reduced to favor more extractable and insertional Na<sup>+</sup>. The reinforced MnO<sub>6</sub>-octahedra slabs are beneficial to the structure stability of P2-Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. In situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) indicates that there is no inferior phase transition at the high charge voltages during cycles. It turns out to exhibit a high specific capacity of 240.5 mAh  g<sup>−1</sup> with Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> as the redox couple and the highest energy density of 654 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> with good cycle stability and rate capability.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Results and discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Characterization and structure analysis</h3><p>Na<sub>0.67−<i>x</i></sub>K<sub><i>x</i></sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (0 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 0.2) was synthesized via a solid-state reaction at 900 °C in air, however, the pure P2 phase was only obtained with <i>x</i> ≤ 0.1, as confirmed by the XRD patterns in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig1">1a</a> and Supplementary Figs.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">2</a>. The optimized composition was found with nominal <i>x</i> = 0.1. Its exact composition was determined to P2-Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> by inductively coupled plasma-optical emission spectrometry (ICP-OES) in Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">1</a>. The oxygen non-stoichiometry in the as-synthesized P2-Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2-δ</sub> was determined by iodine stoichiometry titration. As listed in Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">2</a>, it shows the atomic ratio of oxygen is 1.985, which is mainly attributed to oxygen vacancies on its surface<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Song, J. et al. Role of oxygen vacancies on the performance of Li[Ni0.5-xMn1.5+x]O4 (x = 0, 0.05, and 0.08) spinel cathodes for lithium-ion batteries. Chem. Mater. 24, 3101–3109 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e897">26</a></sup>. The XRD pattern and Rietveld refinement of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> are displayed in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig1">1a</a> and Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">3</a>, and all the diffraction peaks are assigned to a hexagonal lattice with a space group of <i>P6</i><sub><i>3</i></sub><i>/mmc</i>. The refined structure of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is depicted along the <i>y</i> and <i>z</i> axes in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig1">1b, c</a>, respectively. There exist two types of trigonal prismatic sites for Na<sup>+</sup>, which are sharing faces (Na<sub>f</sub>) and edges (Na<sub>e</sub>) with the MnO<sub>6</sub>-octahedra in the two adjacent slabs, respectively, as depicted in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig1">1c</a> and Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">4</a>. The two adjacent Na sites are not accessible at the same time, because of their large electrostatic repulsion. Rietveld refinement indicates that K<sup>+</sup> is located at the Na<sub>e</sub> site (Supplementary Tables&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">4</a>), which is mainly ascribed to the much higher stability of the Na<sub>e</sub> sites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Clément, R. J. et al. Structurally stable Mg-doped P2-Na2/3Mn1-yMgyO2 sodium-ion battery cathodes with high rate performance: insights from electrochemical, NMR and diffraction studies. Energy Environ. Sci. 9, 3240–3251 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e970">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Bianchini, M., Wang, J., Clément, R. &amp; Ceder, G. A first-principles and experimental investigation of nickel solubility into the P2 NaxCoO2 sodium-ion cathode. Adv. Energy Mater. 8, 1801446 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e973">28</a></sup>. Moreover, the interlayer space (<i>d</i><sub>(Na-O-Na)</sub>) is enlarged to 3.58 Å in Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (3.42 Å in Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>) due to the larger ionic radii for K<sup>+</sup> versus Na<sup>+</sup>. On the contrary, the <i>d</i><sub>MnO2</sub> is reduced to 2.02 Å in Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (2.28 Å in Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>), which is resulted from the enhanced MnO<sub>2</sub>-slabs. Thus, the distances of adjacent MnO<sub>2</sub>-slabs (<i>d</i>-spacing) are reduced in K-doped compounds even with larger-sized K<sup>+</sup> in the Na layers.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Crystal structure of Na0.612K0.056MnO2."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Crystal structure of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-021-22523-3/MediaObjects/41467_2021_22523_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig1" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-021-22523-3/MediaObjects/41467_2021_22523_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure 1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="353"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> XRD pattern and Rietveld refinement. Typical layered structure of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> viewed along the <i>y</i> axis (<b>b</b>) and the <i>z</i> axis (<b>c</b>) with K<sup>+</sup> located at the Na<sub>e</sub> sites. <b>d</b> PDF pattern and structure of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. The representative peaks correspond to the bond length of Mn–O (blue line) and Mn–Mn (pale blue line) as labeled. <b>e</b> Fitting of Mn K-edge FT-EXAFS spectra of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 1" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Synchrotron X-ray pair distribution function (PDF) analysis was performed to study the local structure of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig1">1d</a>. The P2-type layered structure is consistent with XRD refinement, as depicted in Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">5</a>. The Mn–O and Mn–Mn bond lengths are 1.890 and 2.891 Å, respectively, which are shorter than those in Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (1.897 and 2.901) in Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">5</a> and Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">6</a>. This suggests the strengthened chemical bonds and reinforced interaction of MnO<sub>6</sub>-octahedra slabs. X-ray adsorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectra in Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">6a</a> imply that Mn has the approximately same average oxidation states in Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. The fitted Mn K-edge Fourier-transformed extended X-ray adsorption fine structure (FT-EXAFS) spectrum of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig1">1e</a>, as well as Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> in Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">6b</a>. As listed in Supplementary Tables&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">8</a>, it shows shortened bond lengths of Mn–O (1.889 Å, 1.895 Å for Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>) and Mn–Mn (2.889 Å, 2.897 Å for Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>). This is in good agreement with the PDF results and the <i>d</i>-spacings of 0.56 and 0.57 nm between the (002) facets in Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, respectively, as observed by high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) in Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">7</a>.</p><p>Scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) equipped with the detectors of high-angle annular dark field (HAADF) and annular bright field (ABF) are employed to study the atomic structure of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig2">2a, b</a> and Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">8</a>, which is 1–10 μm in size in Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">9a</a>. The bright dots in the STEM-HAADF images and the dark dots in STEM-ABF image are assigned to the atomic columns of Mn, between which are the atomic columns of Na or K. The STEM-HAADF and SEM images in Supplementary Figs.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">9b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">10</a> indicate that Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> has similar morphology and layered structure with Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. Electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS) in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig2">2c</a> and Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">11</a> were collected in the selected areas as indicated, in which the peaks of K L-edge at 297 eV indicate the presence of K in the Na layers in Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Atomic structure and elemental distribution of Na0.612K0.056MnO2."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Atomic structure and elemental distribution of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-021-22523-3/MediaObjects/41467_2021_22523_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig2" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-021-22523-3/MediaObjects/41467_2021_22523_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure 2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="426"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> STEM-HAADF image along the direction of [010] of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. <b>b</b> Magnified images of the indicated squares in <b>a</b>. <b>c</b> EELS spectrum of the selected area in the inset image. <b>d</b> SIMS elemental profiles at different sputtering times. <b>e</b> Solid-state <sup>39</sup>K NMR spectra of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and K<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 2" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Dynamic secondary ion mass spectroscopy (SIMS) is performed to analyze the distribution of elements from the surface to the bulk of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig2">2d</a>, as well as their elemental mappings in Supplementary Figs.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">13</a>. It is shown that the contents of K and Na are increased along with a slight decrease of Mn in the initial 50 s of sputtering, and then they are gradually dropped and become constant. The K profile shows a slight aggregation trend on the surface in the initial 50 s of sputtering, and then the intensity ratio of K and Na is gradually decreased to a stable value, indicating uniform distribution of K and Na in the bulk of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. Similar trends for Na, Mn, and O are observed in the SIMS spectra of Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> in Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">14</a>. Solid-state <sup>39</sup>K nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectrum of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> with K<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> as a reference is shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig2">2e</a>. The peak at ~0 ppm is assigned to K<sup>+</sup> located in the K-layers in the crystal structure of K<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. The other two weak peaks at 70 and −70 ppm appear symmetrically and are assigned to the spinning sidebands due to the rotation of sample tube<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Scheler, U., Blümich, B. &amp; Spiess, H. W. Spinning sidebands from chemical shift anisotropy in 13C MAS imaging. Solid State Nucl. Magn. Reson. 2, 105–110 (1993)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e1353">29</a></sup>. A negative chemical shift is observed for Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, which is attributed to the smaller electronic shielding effect of Na and O in Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> than K and O in K<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, and a larger <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.64 nm in K<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> than in P2-Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> with the similar layered structure, as shown in Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">15</a> and Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">9</a><sub>.</sub> These imply that K<sup>+</sup> exists only in the Na layer, not in the MnO<sub>2</sub>-layer, and has a uniform distribution in the bulk particles of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Reaction mechanism</h3><p>The structure evolution of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> upon Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and intercalation is monitored by in situ XRD in a home-made battery within a voltage window of 1.8–4.3 V. As shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig3">3a</a>, each XRD pattern is obtained during the battery operation and corresponding to the state as indicated in the charge and discharge curves in the right-hand panel. The pristine Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is identified by the characteristic diffraction peaks of the (002), (004), (100), (102), and (104) planes in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig3">3a</a>. The P2 phase of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is sustained in the first charge process without phase transition, and 0.498 Na<sup>+</sup> per formula is extracted to give the fully charged state (I) of Na<sub>0.114</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, which agrees well with the ICP-OES in Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">1</a>. In the following discharge process, the P2 phase of the host is kept until 0.536 Na<sup>+</sup> is inserted for Na<sub>0.65</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (II). Due to the slight distortion of the MnO<sub>6</sub>-octahedra slabs, the P’2 phase starts to appear with the co-existence of P2 phase, and becomes dominant in the fully discharged state (III) of Na<sub>1.015</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. In the reverse charge process, the P’2 phase is gradually transformed to the P2 phase (IV), and then it is sustained at high charge voltages till 4.3 V (V). It involves the reversible two-phase transition of P2 ↔ P′2 with total insertion or extraction of 0.901 Na<sup>+</sup> in the cycles of discharge and charge, as schematically illustrated in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig3">3b</a>. In contrast, P2-Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> involves large gliding of the MnO<sub>6</sub>-octahedra slabs for the phase transition of P2 → OP4 upon extraction of 0.455 Na<sup>+</sup> at high charge voltages; it then converts from the OP4 phase into P2 and P′2 during discharge with total insertion or extraction of 0.671 Na<sup>+</sup> in cycles, as shown in the schematic phase transitions of P2-Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig3">3c</a> and in situ XRD patterns in Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">17</a>. Rietveld refinements of the fully charged and discharged Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and P2-Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (Supplementary Figs.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">18</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">21</a> and Supplementary Tables&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">10</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">13</a>) show drastic changes in the crystal structure and lattice parameters of P2-Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. It was found that the volume variation of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>K<sub>0.065</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is 7.8%, which is significantly smaller than 11.2% in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, and favors the structural stability during cycling. It reveals that more Na<sup>+</sup> (0.901 vs. 0.671 Na<sup>+</sup>) can be extracted at the high charge voltages and re-inserted from/into P2-Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> than P2-Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, due to the reduced Na-O bond strength and reinforced MnO<sub>6</sub>-octahedra slabs for suppression of the inferior OP4 phase.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Structure evolution during charge and discharge."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Structure evolution during charge and discharge.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-021-22523-3/MediaObjects/41467_2021_22523_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig3" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-021-22523-3/MediaObjects/41467_2021_22523_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure 3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="487"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> In situ XRD patterns corresponding to the charge and discharge curves of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. Schematic illustration of phase transitions along with the content change of Na<sup>+</sup> in Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (<b>b</b>) and Na<sub>0706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (<b>c</b>) in cycles of charge and discharge.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 3" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The redox of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is further unveiled by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and EELS. The XPS Mn 2<i>p</i> spectrum of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig4">4a</a> shows the splitting Mn 2<i>p</i><sub>3/2</sub> and Mn 2<i>p</i><sub>1/2</sub> at 640.8/642.2 and 652.4/654.9 eV, respectively, suggesting the co-existence of Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup> with a ratio of 2:1. Upon charging, the intensity of Mn<sup>4+</sup> peaks increases with decrease of the intensity of Mn<sup>3+</sup> peaks, indicating oxidation of Mn<sup>3+</sup> to Mn<sup>4+</sup> in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig4">4a</a>. The remaining Mn<sup>3+</sup> is 0.08 per formula at the charge finish of 4.3 V, which is consistent with the phase transition in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig3">3b</a>. The reduction of Mn<sup>4+</sup> to Mn<sup>3+</sup> in the following discharge process is evidenced by the gradual intensity increase of Mn<sup>3+</sup> peaks till the disappearance of Mn<sup>4+</sup> peaks, as shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig4">4b</a>. The peaks at 646.5 eV in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig4">4a, b</a> are mainly attributed to Augers of C–F and Na–F<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Kövér, L. et al. Chemical effects on F KLL Auger spectra in fluorides. J. Vac. Sci. Technol. A 19, 1143–1149 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e1673">30</a></sup>. In addition, the EELS spectra of the charged, discharged, and pristine states of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig4">4c</a> further confirm the redox of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> with the reversible shifts of the two peaks of Mn-L<sub>2</sub> and Mn-L<sub>3</sub>. It is notable that there is no peak shift or broadening in both of the O pre-peak and K-edge, which suggests no valence change of O<sup>2-</sup>. Therefore, the redox of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> is responsible for the charge compensation in the extraction and reinsertion of Na<sup>+</sup> from/into Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> in the discharge and charge processes. Also, no oxygen evolution was detected by in situ differential electrochemical mass spectrometry (DEMS) in the charge process (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">22</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Valence evolution during charge and discharge of Na0.612K0.056MnO2."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Valence evolution during charge and discharge of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-021-22523-3/MediaObjects/41467_2021_22523_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig4" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-021-22523-3/MediaObjects/41467_2021_22523_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure 4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="878"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>XPS Mn 2<i>p</i> spectra at the charge (<b>a</b>) and discharge (<b>b</b>) states. <b>c</b> EELS spectra at different states of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 4" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations using Vienna Ab-initio Simulation Package (VASP) are performed to optimize the crystal structures of the pristine and charged cathode materials<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Perdew, J. P., Burke, K. &amp; Ernzerhof, M. Generalized gradient approximation made simple. Phys. Rev. Lett. 77, 3865–3868 (1996)." href="#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e1763">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kresse, G. &amp; Furthmüller, J. Efficient iterative schemes for ab initio total-energy calculations using a plane-wave basis set. J. Phys. Rev. B 54, 11169–11186 (1996)." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e1763_1">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Blöchl, P. E. Projector augmented-wave method. Phys. Rev. B 50, 17953–17979 (1994)." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e1763_2">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Kresse, G. &amp; Joubert, D. From ultrasoft pseudopotentials to the projector augmented-wave method. Phys. Rev. B 59, 1758–1775 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e1766">34</a></sup>. Because the numbers of K and Na atoms can only be integral in a unit cell, there exists a slight difference between the simulated formula and the obtained composition, which can be reasonably ignored. Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig5">5a</a> and Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">23</a> exhibit the first-principle molecular dynamics simulations of Na<sub>0.500</sub>K<sub>0.055</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.555</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, respectively, where the mean square displacements (MSDs) from 0 to 5 ps reflect the diffusion capability of each element. It shows that Na<sup>+</sup> is more facile to migrate in Na<sub>0.500</sub>K<sub>0.055</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, and K<sup>+</sup> is immobile as riveted between layers. Its migration is energetically unfavorable as displayed in Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">24</a>, in which its migration energy barrier is as high as ~0.8 eV in contrast with ~0.4 eV for Na<sup>+</sup> in Na<sub>0.500</sub>K<sub>0.055</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kang, K., Meng, Y. S., Bréger, J., Grey, C. P. &amp; Ceder, G. Electrodes with high power and high capacity for rechargeable lithium batteries. Science 311, 977–980 (2006)." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e1810">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, X. et al. Super charge separation and high voltage phase in NaxMnO2. Adv. Funct. Mater. 28, 1805105 (2018)." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e1810_1">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Li, X. et al. Jahn-Teller assisted Na diffusion for high performance Na ion batteries. Chem. Mater. 28, 6575–6583 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e1813">37</a></sup>). Its diffusion coefficient is accordingly five orders of magnitude lower than that of Na<sup>+</sup> based on Arrhenius equation. This is consistent with ICP-OES and elemental mappings in Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">1</a> and Figs.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">25</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">26</a>, in which the content of K remains almost constant, suggesting no K loss in the charge and discharge processes. The optimized Na<sub>0.500</sub>K<sub>0.055</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> in Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">27a</a> shows a pure P2 phase with K<sup>+</sup> located in the Na layers. It can be sustained in the optimized structure of P2-Na<sub>0.220</sub>K<sub>0.055</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> at the fully charged state with similar structure parameters in Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">27b</a>. This indicates the significant role of K for the stabilization of the crystal structure of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> for extraction and insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>. It is robust to allow for the extraction of 0.498 Na<sup>+</sup> in the first charge without phase transition, as depicted in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig3">3</a>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Simulated atomic and electronic structures."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Simulated atomic and electronic structures.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-021-22523-3/MediaObjects/41467_2021_22523_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig5" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-021-22523-3/MediaObjects/41467_2021_22523_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure 5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="571"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b> MSD of Mn, K, Na, and O as functions of time in Na<sub>0.500</sub>K<sub>0.055</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. <b>b</b> Total density of states (tDOS) of Na<sub>0.220</sub>K<sub>0.055</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and partial density of states (pDOS) of Na 3 <i>s</i>, K 4 <i>s</i>, O 2<i>p</i> and Mn 3<i>d</i> orbitals, as labeled by yellow, purple, red and green, respectively. The Fermi energy (<i>E</i><sub>f</sub>) is set to 0 eV, and the pDOS of Na 3 <i>s</i>, K 4 <i>s</i> are enlarged by 100 times. <b>c</b> Partial charge density distribution along the blue plane in the left panel for Na<sub>0.500</sub>K<sub>0.055</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (visualized by Visualization for Electronic and Structure Analysis (VESTA)). The red regions represent high charge density, and the blue regions indicate low charge density. <b>d</b> Plot of deformation charge densities of Na<sub>0.500</sub>K<sub>0.055</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. The charge accumulation region is rendered in yellow, and the charge depletion is in blue. <b>e</b> Schematic pDOS of Na<sub>0.555</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.500</sub>K<sub>0.055</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. <b>f</b> COHP analysis on representative prismatic sites and the adjacent MnO<sub>6</sub>-octahedra sites of Na<sub>0.555</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.500</sub>K<sub>0.055</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 5" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The electronic structures of Na<sub>0.500</sub>K<sub>0.055</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.555</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> have been investigated by DFT calculations. As shown in the density of states (DOS) of Na<sub>0.220</sub>K<sub>0.055</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig5">5b</a>, although K only accounts for one fourth of Na in the composition, the DOS contributed by K atoms is similar to that of Na atoms. In addition, the partial charge density distribution in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig5">5c</a> reflects the local status of electrons. The electron cloud overlap between K and O is stronger than that between Na and O. As seen from the deformation charge densities for Na<sub>0.500</sub>K<sub>0.055</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig5">5d</a>, the electron population at O atoms increases. However, the crystal orbital Hamilton populations (COHP) in Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">28</a> indicate that it falls in a K-O antibonding region around the Fermi level, which is different from the bonding area of Na–O in Na<sub>0.555</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. Schematic pDOS of Na<sub>0.500</sub>K<sub>0.055</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.555</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> are shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig5">5e</a> and Supplementary Figs.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">29</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">30</a>. The increased spin-down electrons around Fermi energy indicate stronger hybridization interaction between Mn and O in the presence of K<sup>+</sup>, in good agreement with the analysis of FT-EXAFS in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig1">1d</a>. Also, the Na-O bond strength in Na<sub>0.500</sub>K<sub>0.055</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is found to be reduced by COHP analysis in Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">14</a>. As indicated by COHP analysis in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig5">5f</a> and Supplementary Tables&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">15</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">17</a>, the Mn–O bonds are reinforced for both adjacent and distant Mn atoms with a more obvious increase in <i>xy</i>-plane by the substitution of K. A slight fluctuation of Mn–O bond strength is found for different valence state of Mn. These lead to more thermodynamically favorable Na<sup>+</sup> vacancies (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">18</a>), which is the determining factor for Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and insertion, consistent with MSD in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig5">5a</a>. Of note, the formation energy for K<sup>+</sup> siting in the Na<sub>e</sub> site (0.675 eV) is higher than in the Na<sub>f</sub> site (−3.500 eV) of Na<sub>0.500</sub>K<sub>0.055</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>, which indicates the higher thermodynamic stability of the Na<sub>e</sub> site for K<sup>+</sup>. Furthermore, the reinforced Mn–O bonds play an important role to suppress the phase transition during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction and insertion and favor the cycle stability.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Electrochemical performance</h3><p>The electrochemical performance of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is evaluated against Na metal anodes with the electrolyte of 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC) and 5 wt.% of fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) in coin cells. Figure&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig6">6a</a> exhibits the typical charge and discharge profiles of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> delivers a high initial charge capacity of 125.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to 0.498 Na<sup>+</sup> per formula. In the following cycles, the reversible capacity of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> reaches up to 240.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which is much higher than that of Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (167.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). This means that 0.901 Na<sup>+</sup> per formula is inserted and extracted into/from Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub>. It agrees well with the restricted phase transition in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig3">3</a>. At the same time, Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> presents higher discharge voltages than Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> does, which is consistent with the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves in Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#MOESM1">31</a>. The energy density of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is as high as 654 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, in comparison with the reported cathode materials in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#Fig6">6b</a>. Of particular, it is even higher than that of Na<sub>0.72</sub>Li<sub>0.24</sub>Mn<sub>0.76</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, which works with both the O redox and Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> to deliver the highest specific capacity in refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, Y. M. et al. Air-stable copper-based P2-Na7/9Cu2/9Fe1/9Mn2/3O2 as a new positive electrode material for sodium-ion batteries. Adv. Sci. 2, 1500031 (2015)." href="#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e2206">38</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Talaie, E., Duffort, V., Smith, H. L., Fultz, B. &amp; Nazar, L. F. Structure of the high voltage phase of layered P2-Na2/3-z[Mn1/2Fe1/2]O2 and the positive effect of Ni substitution on its stability. Energy Environ. Sci. 8, 2512–2523 (2015)." href="#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e2206_1">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rong, X. H. et al. Anionic redox reaction-induced high-capacity and low-strain cathode with suppressed phase transition. Joule 3, 503–517 (2019)." href="#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e2206_2">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ma, C. et al. Soc. 139, 4835–4845 (2017)." href="#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e2206_3">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Luo, C., Langrock, A., Fan, X., Liang, Y. &amp; Wang, C. P2-type transition metal oxides for high performance Na-ion battery cathodes. J. Mater. Chem. A 5, 18214–18220 (2017)." href="#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e2206_4">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yabuuchi, Y. et al. New O2/P2-type Li-excess layered manganese oxides as promising multi-functional electrode materials for rechargeable Li/Na batteries. Adv. Energy Mater. 4, 1301453 (2014)." href="#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e2206_5">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jian, Z. et al. Atomic structure and kinetics of NASICON NaxV2(PO4)3 cathode for sodium-ion batteries. Adv. Funct. Mater. 24, 4265–4272 (2014)." href="#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e2206_6">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Kang, W. et al. Copper substituted P2-type Na0.67CuxMn1-xO2: a stable high-power sodium-ion battery cathode. J. Mater. Chem. A 3, 22846–22852 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d1718850e2209">45</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Electrochemical performance."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Electrochemical performance.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-021-22523-3/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-021-22523-3/MediaObjects/41467_2021_22523_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig6" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-021-22523-3/MediaObjects/41467_2021_22523_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure 6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="630"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b> Typical charge/discharge curves of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.706</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup> in the third cycle. <b>b</b> Comparison of energy density and average voltages of Na<sub>0.612</sub>K<sub>0.056</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> with typical cathode materials in refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Li, Y. M. et al. Air-stable copper-based P2-Na7/9Cu2/9Fe1/9Mn2/3O2 as a new positive electrode material for sodium-ion batteries. Adv.
